"order": 9}, {"amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "display": true, "new_amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "order": 10}], "adsOrder": [2]}
        </script><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>By Claire Wordley</p><p>The rancher peers at an enormous bird high in the treetops. Reining in his horse, he squints upward. It's not moving; maybe it will still be there when he gets back. An hour later, he returns with a gun. The huge animal is still there, motionless. Taking aim, he fires, and the heavy body comes crashing to the ground. He picks it up, marveling at its size and the incredible crest of feathers around its head. He strokes the soft gray and white plumes, perhaps feeling a sudden pang of sadness that the proud head is now lolling lifeless, as he puts the carcass over his saddle to show his family. </p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-1" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="1" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem1">&lt;p&gt;This scenario and others like it are impacting harpy eagle (&lt;em&gt;Harpia harpyja&lt;/em&gt;) populations across Central and South America, according to a &lt;a href="https://meridian.allenpress.com/rapt/article-abstract/doi/10.3356/JRR-20-76/462112/Human-Persecution-of-the-Harpy-Eagle-A-Widespread?redirectedFrom=PDF" target="_blank"&gt;scientific paper&lt;/a&gt; recently published in the &lt;em&gt;Journal of Raptor Research&lt;/em&gt;. Despite being the largest raptor in South America and the national bird of Panama, scientists know little about the extent to which the species is persecuted by humans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the study, the researchers found a total of 132 documented cases where a harpy eagle was killed or captured between 1950 and 2020, including 21 cases from Colombia and Panama that had never before been published in scientific journals.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-2" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="2" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem2">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" data-runner-src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTYxOS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTkxNjA2M30.r9Qk_WEbtSQGa1-pmE1B587T9tlZHPZ01RhYu01eFBU/img.png?width=980" id="ab195" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="f837a22cadd69b1cb8c0d396cfe37454" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image"   data-width="1128" data-height="787" /&gt;<small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p>Harpy eagle. Image by Jonathan Wilkins via <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Harpy_Eagle_with_wings_lifted.jpg" target="_blank">Wikimedia Commons</a> / <a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-sa/3.0/" target="_blank">CC BY-SA 3.0</a><span></span></p></small></div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-3" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="3" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem3">&lt;p&gt;"This story can be used to repair what has been done to this magnificent species over the last seven decades," said Helena Aguiar-Silva, of the University of So Paulo and &lt;a href="https://www.projetoharpia.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Projeto Harpia&lt;/a&gt;, Brazil, one of the scientists who compiled the persecution data set.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The harpy eagle's range stretches from Guatemala and Belize in Central America, down into South America to Bolivia and Paraguay. Occasionally the birds are noted as far north as Mexico or as far south as Argentina.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Aguiar-Silva said there probably used to be stable harpy eagle populations in both those countries, but they have been driven locally extinct by the expansion of hunting and farming, as they were in El Salvador.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While the species' range spans more than half a continent, that doesn't mean it's abundant. Surveys show that it is never locally common. The bird is slow to reach sexual maturity, raising just one chick per pair every two or three years, and the number of harpy eagles is &lt;a href="https://www.iucnredlist.org/species/22695998/117357127" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;decreasing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Aguiar-Silva said the declines and local extinctions, combined with the evidence that it is frequently persecuted, should serve as a basis to reanalyze the global conservation status of the species. While the harpy eagle's current conservation status on the &lt;a href="https://www.iucnredlist.org/species/22695998/117357127" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;IUCN Red List&lt;/a&gt; is "near threatened" at the international level, individual countries have placed it in more critical categories: it is classified as vulnerable in countries including &lt;a href="https://www.nationalredlist.org/search2/species-search/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Brazil&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://www.minam.gob.pe/simposio-peruano-de-especies-cites/wp-content/uploads/sites/157/2018/08/Listado-FAUNA-CITES-FINAL.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Peru&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://www.nationalredlist.org/species-information/?speciesID=73158" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Venezuela&lt;/a&gt;, and critically endangered in &lt;a href="https://www.nationalredlist.org/species-information/?speciesID=232370" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Nicaragua&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-4" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="4" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem4">&lt;h2&gt;Why People Kill Harpy Eagles&lt;/h2&gt;&lt;p&gt;A female harpy eagle's wingspan can reach 224 centimeters (7.3 feet) and they can weigh up to 9 kilograms or 20 pounds in the wild, making them an impressive sight. Dietary analyses, including by &lt;a href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/260229205_Food_Habits_of_the_Harpy_Eagle_a_Top_Predator_from_the_Amazonian_Rainforest_Canopy" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Aguiar-Silva&lt;/a&gt;, have shown that harpy eagles mostly eat wild animals that live in trees. Sloths are by far their most important food source, but &lt;a href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/282461036_Conservation_implications_of_harpy_eagle_Harpia_harpyja_predation_patterns" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;monkeys also make a popular meal&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Harpy eagles' role in killing leaf-eating animals and omnivores like capuchin monkeys are important in maintaining the rainforest ecosystem. According to the &lt;a href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/260229205_Food_Habits_of_the_Harpy_Eagle_a_Top_Predator_from_the_Amazonian_Rainforest_Canopy" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;research so far&lt;/a&gt;, harpy eagles only occasionally &lt;a href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/267150702_Aves_de_Rapina_Raras_no_Estado_de_Mato_Grosso_do_Sul_Brasil" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;eat livestock&lt;/a&gt;, probably in part because they carry their food to trees to eat. Even females, which can be double the weight of a male, &lt;a href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/282461036_Conservation_implications_of_harpy_eagle_Harpia_harpyja_predation_patterns" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;rarely prey on animals heavier than 5 kg (11 lb)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-5" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="5" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem5">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" data-runner-src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTYyNC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMDMwMjI3MX0._AinLbLrKUrLb4FW8ElTaK2v-zlH-T3ABeyCq5Mh25s/img.png?width=980" id="007d1" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="5c5b6457beebe9954ff402300bf5ad82" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image"   data-width="1129" data-height="636" /&gt;<small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p>Harpy eagle chick in its nest. Everton Miranda</p></small></div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-6" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="6" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem6">&lt;p&gt;However, the rarity with which the eagles carry off farm animals has not stopped people from hunting them. Everton Miranda, a Brazilian scientist affiliated with the University of KwaZulu-Natal, South Africa, has studied why people hunt harpy eagles in Brazil. His research, investigating more than 180 harpy eagle killings over two years in the state of Mato Grosso, will soon be published in the journal &lt;em&gt;Animal Conservation&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He told Mongabay that people usually shoot the eagles "out of curiosity."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"People see this large raptor  they frequently don't know what it is  and shoot it to have a closer look," he said. "One trait that makes harpy eagles unfortunately prone to being killed  is that they remain perched on a single tree for several hours or even a whole day."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This behavior gives the hunter the time to travel home for their gun and return to shoot the animal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to the interviews he conducted, most of the people who killed the eagles out of curiosity later expressed regret about it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This surprising finding  that curiosity and a desire to see the birds close up could account for up to 80% of harpy killings in some places  echoes results from &lt;a href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/242218718_Curiosity_killed_the_bird_arbitrary_hunting_of_Harpy_Eagles_Harpia_harpyja_on_an_agricultural_frontier_in_southern_Brazilian_Amazonia" target="_blank"&gt;an earlier Brazilian study&lt;/a&gt;. According to Miranda's research, prevention or retaliation for livestock predation represented only 20% of harpy eagle killings in his study site.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-7" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="7" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem7">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" data-runner-src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTYyOS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzOTM1MjUyOX0.TJovv636BFvsUjL5AOnlDKKSjHVc41QeXTXf09dhIHw/img.png?width=980" id="290c8" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="d2457101c1898d8741ed8eb378467115" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image"   data-width="903" data-height="679" /&gt;<small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p>Hunters with a dead harpy eagle. Everton Miranda</p></small></div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-8" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="8" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem8">&lt;p&gt;Of course, the reasons behind such killings are likely to vary over the harpy eagle's range.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Santiago Zuluaga, senior author of the paper in Journal of &lt;em&gt;Raptor Research&lt;/em&gt;, told Mongabay that in his experience some people hunt harpy eagles for food, while others capture them live for illegal sale. A &lt;a href="https://www.elespectador.com/noticias/bogota/bogota-se-logra-la-incautacion-de-dos-garras-de-aguila-y-un-cuerno-de-antilope/" target="_blank"&gt;recent killing in Colombia&lt;/a&gt; was to sell the bird's feathers and claws on the black market.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"There is also a story of an eagle that was hunted to obtain one of the claws that is now used to baptize children, which is supposed to give them luck and strength for the rest of their lives," said Zuluaga, with the Colaboratorio de Biodiversidad, Ecologa y Conservacin (ColBEC), Argentina, and the &lt;a href="http://www.proyectoaguilacrestada.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Fundacin Proyecto guila Crestada-Colombia&lt;/a&gt;, Colombia. "However, it is a story and we have not confirmed it."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Mateo Giraldo-Amaya at EAFIT University, Colombia, who led the &lt;em&gt;Journal of Raptor Research&lt;/em&gt; paper, the cases they found are "only the tip of an iceberg."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"I believe that many more eagles are being killed today in Colombia and the Neotropics, but that this information usually does not come to light because of the nature of the events and the people who perpetrate them," he added.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Notably, researchers found no records of raptor persecution in seven of the 18 countries inhabited by harpy eagles, including Bolivia, Paraguay and Peru. But this may not be the good news it appears to be. Researchers say the lack of hunting records is probably because there are no dedicated harpy eagle research or conservation programs in those countries, meaning there is nobody to document kills.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-9" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="9" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem9">&lt;h2&gt;Raptor Conservation&lt;/h2&gt;&lt;p&gt;Zuluaga said that even in countries with harpy conservation programs, like Panama and Brazil, people still shoot the eagles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In Panama, Karla Aparicio, another co-author on the paper, has been working to understand and conserve harpy eagles since 1994. In 2015, she set up the &lt;a href="https://www.naturalezayciencia507.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Fundacin Naturaleza y Ciencia 507&lt;/a&gt; to focus on researching and conserving birds of prey.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Following the lead of Aguiar-Silva at Projeto Harpia in Brazil, the 507 team, named after the international dialing code for Panama, set up the first camera traps to monitor harpy eagle nests in the country. As well as studying the species in the wild, they established a center for rescued birds of prey. Birds that can't be reintroduced to the wild become ambassadors for their environmental education program, #HarpyS-cool.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Aparicio said she's committed to teaching people about birds of prey. She knows better than most how, given the right opportunities, even harpy hunters can change their ways: her assistant of 20 years, Euriato Bainora, used to hunt harpy eagles but now works to conserve them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Soon after Aparicio set up the 507 foundation in Panama, a chance event set Giraldo-Amaya on a path to replicate her work in Colombia.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As an undergraduate in 2016, Giraldo-Amaya had a run-in with a harpy eagle that stuck with him. At first, he was excited when his fellow students showed him a harpy eagle nest that they found with an Indigenous guide, Antonio Cunampia, while on a university field trip.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"A week later, we got the news that someone had killed the eagle and its left leg had been cut off," he said. "It was very sad, and we were all extremely anxious about the fate of the chick without its mother."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To check on the chick, the researchers needed a drone. And it turned out undergraduate Giraldo-Amaya had one.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"The greatest coincidence of life," as Giraldo-Amaya put it. "Sadly, the chick died  but this experience marked me."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;His sense of loss over the death of the chick led him to seek out an internship with Aparicio in Panama, which in turn inspired him to co-found the initiative &lt;a href="https://www.facebook.com/PGRColombia/" target="_blank"&gt;Proyecto Grandes Rapaces Colombia&lt;/a&gt; to do similar research and conservation work over the border in Colombia  the first project in the country to focus on harpy eagles.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-10" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="10" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem10"><h3 data-role="headline"></h3>&lt;span style="display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="4fdbaadc49386cf3bd2fbb822dc6cc5a"&gt;&lt;iframe lazy-loadable="true" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/L6gA-y4Xl9o?rel=0" width="100%" height="auto"  frameborder="0" scrolling="no" style="position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;"&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;&lt;/span&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-11" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="11" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem11">&lt;h2&gt;Harpy Eagle Tourism&lt;/h2&gt;&lt;p&gt;In Brazil, scientist and conservationist Miranda has established a collaboration with wildlife tourist company &lt;a href="http://www.southwild.com/the-formula/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;SouthWild&lt;/a&gt;, based around making living harpy eagles a valuable resource for locals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The ecotourism project offers $100 to local people for each harpy eagle nest discovered. The project also hires locals to build platforms so that tourists can see the birds at eye level, and to take care of visitors' needs.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-12" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="12" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem12">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" data-runner-src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTYzOC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzE2NzU0N30.nWFAoIT1Rt7e3c2oT9IIeZYn7aPDeq8rNoyNBzH3M2U/img.png?width=980" id="470e6" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="ddd4036e959799734c346a56bf0b7480" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image"   data-width="374" data-height="771" /&gt;<small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p><em>A harpy eagle chick with a tourist viewing tower in the background in Mato Grosso, Brazil. Everton Miranda</em></p></small></div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-13" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/harpy-eagle-killings" data-id="13" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100499" data-published-at="1615907846" id="rebelltitem13">&lt;p&gt;Miranda said his harpy protection model also conserves the surrounding forest, providing habitat for many other species. Thirty landowners have signed an agreement that includes protecting at least 320 hectares (790 acres) of forest surrounding a harpy nest.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"We pay the landowner $20 per tourist per day," Miranda told Mongabay, noting that this has stopped eagle persecution in those areas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Miranda said protecting the forest is vital, as deforestation remains the other major threat to harpy eagles. He said habitat loss caused by beef and soy farming are harder to address than direct persecution. Making meat production deforestation-free (soy is mostly used to feed farmed animals) requires action at every level, according to Miranda, from local law enforcement to international legislation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But his years of working with the iconic harpy eagle make him determined to give the birds a fighting chance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"There are a few occasions when you feel deep inside you're doing the right thing," he said. "When I first recorded a harpy laying an egg on a camera trap  then I felt heart-warmed by that small light that poets call hope."&lt;/p&gt;</div>

                    &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Reposted with permission from &lt;a href="https://news.mongabay.com/2021/03/dying-of-curiosity-why-people-shoot-harpy-eagles/" target="_blank"&gt;Mongabay&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;<div class="around-the-web"><div class="from-your-site__headline">From Your Site Articles</div>

<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="The New River Gorge: Ancient River, Old Mines, New National Park" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge-2651100360.html">
        The New River Gorge: Ancient River, Old Mines, New National Park
    </a>
</h2><div class="social-date">

<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="The New River Gorge: Ancient River, Old Mines, New National Park" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge-2651100360.html">
        The New River Gorge: Ancient River, Old Mines, New National Park
    </a>
</h2>
<div class="social-author clearfix">
<div class="social-author__avatar image">
<div class="cover-all image" style='background-image: url("https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNDk4NDYwMC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMjAwNzc4Mn0.2l6YjC3tsfCBhNewi4z0uq95XWniTLrhCl39bX-3GVY/img.jpg?width=210")'></div>

<span style="background-color: initial;">From this overlook 1,400 feet above the the river, one can understand why the New River Gorge is known by many as the Grand Canyon of the East. <em>Sky Noir Photography</em></span><em> / Moment / Getty Images</em>

"order": 9}, {"amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "display": true, "new_amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "order": 10}], "adsOrder": [2]}
        </script><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>By Mary Caperton Morton</p><p><span></span>The only thing "new" about West Virginia's New River Gorge is its national park status: In 2021, the New River Gorge became our <a href="https://www.nps.gov/neri/index.htm" target="_blank">latest national park</a>, but in geologic terms, the New River is anything but new. Dating back to the days of the supercontinent Pangaea, it is one of the oldest rivers in the world and one of the few waterways in North America that runs north.</p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template>
                    &lt;p&gt;A lot can happen when a river keeps its course for over 300 million years. The New River's unusual northsouth orientation serves as a corridor for animal migration, fueling a biodiversity hot spot that is home to an impressive array of endemic species, many of them endangered.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Millions of years of fluvial erosion have also carved a deep gorge lined by long tracts of bituminous coal and steep cliffs of quartzite sandstone. In the late 1800s, dozens of coal mines and company towns were built along the New's raging river canyon, accessible only by railroad. Today their vine-covered ruins are being reclaimed by the regenerating forest, and the ecological impacts of mining and deforestation on the New River's watershed are slowly healing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A mere 30 kilometers from the New, however, deep scars blasted into the landscape by modern mountaintop removal mining operations may prove more or less permanent, even on geologic timescales.&lt;/p&gt;
                    
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="new-river-old-course" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="1" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem1"><h3 data-role="headline">New River, Old Course</h3>&lt;p&gt;"The New River might be the most inappropriately named river on Earth," said &lt;a href="https://www.usgs.gov/staff-profiles/nathaniel-hitt?qt-staff_profile_science_products=0#qt-staff_profile_science_products" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Nathaniel Hitt&lt;/a&gt;, a fish biologist with the U.S. Geological Survey in Kearneysville, W.Va.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The New was born when the North American and African plates collided to form the supercontinent Pangaea. The impact uplifted the Appalachian Mountains to Himalayan heights, and a mighty river named the Teays formed to drain the western slopes of the range. From its headwaters in what is now western North Carolina, the &lt;a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1983/11/29/science/a-great-lost-river-gets-its-due.html?&amp;amp;pagewanted=all" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Teays flowed&lt;/a&gt; north through the Virginias before turning west into the Ohio River Basin, eventually draining into a vast inland sea.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today the New River begins in the same headwaters carved by the Teays and follows roughly the same northward path. "These headwaters and this basin have remained in their current configurations for over 300 million years of evolutionary time," Hitt said.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around 60 million years ago, the diagonally trending ridges of the Appalachians went through a period of uplift, but the highly erosive Teays kept cutting into the mountains faster than the uplift rate. Thus, the river's south-to-north course runs counter to the west-to-east flow of most Appalachian waterways, carving a formidable 400-meter-deep river canyon directly through the Appalachian Plateau.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The 580-kilometer-long New River carves the longest and deepest canyon in the Appalachian Mountains; the new 295-square-kilometer national park and preserve is headquartered along an 85-kilometer stretch of the river near Fayetteville, W.Va.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="a-speciation-superhighway" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="2" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem2"><h3 data-role="headline">A Speciation Superhighway</h3>&lt;p&gt;Downstreamto the norththe New joins the Gauley to form the Kanawha River. Just downstream from this confluence, the wide and turbulent Kanawha Falls present a natural barrier to migration, leading to many aquatic species above and below the falls being genetically distinct from one another.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For instance, "when you look at the fish of the New River, you're seeing how evolution plays out over long geologic timescales," Hitt said. "The New River highlights the potential of deep evolutionary time to produce uniquely adapted species." The New River Basin is home to seven endemic species of fishfish that are found nowhere else on Earth. These fish are uniquely adapted to thrive in turbulent white water and have lived through several major extinction events and an ice age that froze the northern reaches of their habitat.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The New's deep canyons also function as a biogeographical corridor for terrestrial animals, facilitating northsouth movement in a landscape where most valleys run eastwest. The New River Gorge's unusual orientation "lies at the core of one of the largest intact temperate forests in the world," said &lt;a href="https://ecosystems.psu.edu/directory/dmanning" target="_blank"&gt;Douglas Manning&lt;/a&gt;, a terrestrial ecologist at the national park. "The gorge has a lot of niche habitats for different organisms to occupy," supporting a diverse assemblage of plants, mammals, birds, and aquatic species.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Whereas the New River allows for northsouth migration, its steep and rugged topography presents a significant barrier to eastwest movement. Historically, even humans have found the New a formidable obstacle. The turbulent waters are not safe for most river-based transportation, and steep cliffs can impede navigation on foot or horseback.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although people have been living in southern West Virginia for at least 11,000 years, the main travel routes of early Indigenous communities "were not through the gorge, because it's so circuitous and dangerous to travel along the river," said Dave Fuerst, cultural resource program manager for the park. "They were using routes that went around the gorge, following ridges and stream drainages." Communities thrived in the ecologically rich area, however, and today more than 400 archaeological sites are documented along the New, Gauley, and Bluestone Rivers, connected by an elaborate network of footpaths.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It wasn't until the completion of the &lt;a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/piedmont_fossil/16210658540" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Chesapeake and Ohio (C&amp;amp;O) Railway&lt;/a&gt; in 1873 that people were able to travel the length of the gorge efficiently. Even then, eastwest travel across the gorge remained harrowing until 1977, when the &lt;a href="https://www.nps.gov/neri/planyourvisit/nrgbridge.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;New River Gorge Bridge&lt;/a&gt; was completed just east of Fayetteville, shortening the cross-gorge travel time from over an hour to less than a minute. This impressive 923-meter-long steel arched span soars 267 meters above the New, making it one of the highest vehicular bridges in the world.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="coal-mining-in-my-mitochondria" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="3" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem3"><h3 data-role="headline">Coal Mining in My Mitochondria</h3>&lt;p&gt;The rock layers exposed by the New River span over 350 million years. Historically, the most sought-after layers in the gorge were bituminous coal, a relatively soft black coal that burns readily, producing little smoke. These layers date to the &lt;a href="https://www.nps.gov/articles/000/pennsylvanian-period.htm" target="_blank"&gt;Pennsylvanian subperiod&lt;/a&gt; of the Carboniferous, when vast swamps covered large regions of the globe, producing thick layers of peat that eventually formed coal.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-4" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="4" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem4">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" data-runner-src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTYwMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MzUyMDkyNX0.zbw4Cb0kSV_kBKJ1p_HrE7FZCIIg8yfw0UzJdhvoTJA/img.jpg?width=980" id="09262" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="101fe9cc469da8214e11bcbee2598c61" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image"   data-width="820" data-height="615" /&gt;<small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p>Coal miners pose on an electric-powered mine locomotive at Kaymoor, W.Va., in 1914. <em><a href="https://www.nps.gov/media/photo/gallery-item.htm?pg=6342271&amp;id=C4424B2A-155D-451F-6798AC7A5B2AB0C2&amp;gid=C3F7F6E7-155D-451F-6787298A742C4106" target="_blank">National Park Service</a></em></p></small>&lt;p&gt;The downward cutting of the New River did a lot of the work to expose bituminous coal seams along the gorge. After the C&amp;amp;O railroad was finished, dozens of coal mines sprang up along the New. From the late 1800s to the mid-1900s, miners hacked millions of tons of coal from the walls of the gorge. The work was brutal, and the pay was around &lt;a href="https://www.nps.gov/neri/learn/historyculture/royal.htm" target="_blank"&gt;45 cents a ton&lt;/a&gt;. Strong miners could earn $2.00 a day.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For a time, New River coal was one of the most abundant on the market, fueling everything from steel mills to power plants. More than 60 coal towns were built along the New River to support the mines. Some of these towns boasted hundreds of buildings and were home to thousands of people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The downward cutting of the New River did a lot of the work to expose bituminous coal seams along the gorge. After the C&amp;amp;O railroad was finished, dozens of coal mines sprang up along the New. From the late 1800s to the mid-1900s, miners hacked millions of tons of coal from the walls of the gorge. The work was brutal, and the pay was around &lt;a href="https://www.nps.gov/neri/learn/historyculture/royal.htm" target="_blank"&gt;45 cents a ton&lt;/a&gt;. Strong miners could earn $2.00 a day.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For a time, New River coal was one of the most abundant on the market, fueling everything from steel mills to power plants. More than 60 coal towns were built along the New River to support the mines. Some of these towns boasted hundreds of buildings and were home to thousands of people.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-5" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="5" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem5">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" data-runner-src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTYxMy9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMTAwODUxMH0.fWlthm3zTHR5UyQPezlsCbvE_SqScnmP8sjsG5XPTmE/img.png?width=980" id="40e4b" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="d6f0505491fc935100d71b0dd7d4c298" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image"   data-width="778" data-height="1122" /&gt;<small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p>In the late 1800s and early 1900s, dozens of coal company mining towns were built along the New River. All of the railroad-accessed towns were abandoned by the 1960s. Coalcampusa.com. </p></small>&lt;p&gt;My family history runs deep in these New River mining towns: The town of Caperton was named for one of my relatives, my maternal great-grandmother was born in Fire Creek, and my paternal grandparents lived and worked in Ames, where New River Gorge Bridge pylons now stand on the east side of the gorge. When my dad was 2 years old, the family moved to Fayetteville, on the west side of the gorge, where my uncle still lives in the family home.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I spent the summers of my childhood exploring the woods and creeks around the gorge, hunting for salamanders and seashell fossils from a long-gone ocean that predates the Appalachians. Every time I visit the New, I feel like a salmon returning to its home stream; I imagine my great-grandmother's mitochondria in my cells vibrating in tune with one of the world's oldest rivers. I've hiked all over the New River Gorge, visiting the overgrown sites and ruins of Ames, Kaymoor, and Nuttallburg, but I have not yet made it farther upriver to Caperton or Fire Creek. Someday an anadromous upriver backpacking trip awaits (although I have no plans to spawn).&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="making-the-leap-from-coal-mining-to-base-jumping" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="6" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem6"><h3 data-role="headline">Making the Leap from Coal Mining to BASE Jumping</h3>&lt;p&gt;All of the mines and coal towns in the New River Gorge were abandoned by the 1960s, and today the still-inaccessible ghost towns are fading into the rapidly regenerating forest.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;"The New River Gorge faces a lot of legacy impacts from all sorts of land use history in the gorge," Manning said. Vast areas of forest were stripped of trees to fuel the coal towns' woodstoves and coke ovens, and timber operations in the gorge continued for decades after mining operations ceased.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"If you look at photos from around the turn of the century and the Great Depression, [you will see that] the forest here was stripped bare," said &lt;a href="https://www.nps.gov/gari/learn/news/eve-west-named-chief-of-interpretation-at-new-river-gorge-national-river-gauley-river-national-recreation-area-and-bluestone-national-scenic-river.htm" target="_blank"&gt;Eve West&lt;/a&gt;, chief of interpretation at the New River Gorge National Park and Preserve. And now, "this is considered to be the most botanically diverse river system in the central and southern Appalachians. There's not a lot of virgin forest here, so that's all come from regrowth in the past 50 years. This place bounces back fast."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Regrowth and resiliency are proving to be a hallmark of this landscape and for its people. Two generations removed from the dark and dangerous coal mines, I find myself drawn upward, to the New River's second most famous rock: Nuttall sandstone, a hard, quartz-rich variety that erodes into vertical cliffs with thin cracks perfect for rock climbing.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-7" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="7" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem7">&lt;p&gt;More than 1,400 traditional and sport climbing routes snake up the walls of the gorge, making it one of the premiere climbing localities in the eastern United States. I cut my teeth on these rocks in more ways than one; in 2008, fresh out of graduate school with a newly minted master's degree in science journalism, one of &lt;a href="https://www.climbing.com/news/a-waste-free-new-river-rendezvous-no-problem/" target="_blank"&gt;my very first assignments&lt;/a&gt; was to cover the &lt;a href="https://www.newriverclimbing.net/" target="_blank"&gt;New River Rendezvous&lt;/a&gt;, an annual 3-day rock climbing festival in the gorge, for &lt;em&gt;Climbing&lt;/em&gt; magazine.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I have not yet rafted the raging rapids of the highly technical New River. Even after braving &lt;a href="https://eos.org/features/will-earths-grandest-canyon-keep-getting-grander" target="_blank"&gt;22 glorious days rafting&lt;/a&gt; the Colorado River through the Grand Canyon, I am still intimidated by the New's legendary hydraulics.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I have even less desire to try the gorge's other adrenaline-fueled sport: BASE (buildings, antennas, spans, and Earth) jumping from the New River Gorge Bridge into the river, 267 meters below. Watching dozens of people eagerly leap from the dizzying span on &lt;a href="https://officialbridgeday.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Bridge Day&lt;/a&gt; is enough of a rush for me.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The New River's potential as an adventurer's paradise is still expanding, with new mountain biking and hiking trails added every year. In 2019, around 1.3 million people recreated at the New, and tourism now drives Fayetteville's once-faltering economy. "The New River Gorge is a fascinating placegeologically, ecologically, and historicallyin a majestic setting," Hitt said. "I hope people will be inspired by this elevated [national park] designation to come visit."&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="we-all-live-downstream" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="8" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem8"><h3 data-role="headline">We All Live Downstream</h3>&lt;p&gt;The New River Gorge National Park and Preserve encompasses almost 295 square kilometers of land around the river corridor, but the New River's entire watershed is more than 18,000 square kilometers. "One of the ongoing challenges is that we can protect and manage the gorge itself, but we don't own the headwaters upstream," where the river is "undoubtedly" still affected by ongoing mining, Manning said.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The New River's upstream watershed includes &lt;a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountaintop_removal_mining" target="_blank"&gt;mountaintop removal mining&lt;/a&gt;, in which the tops of entire mountains are removed to access buried coal seams, and the overburden is pushed into a neighboring valley, destroying thousands of kilometers of mountain streams. Across southern West Virginia, &lt;a href="https://today.duke.edu/2018/07/mapping-mountaintop-coal-mining%E2%80%99s-yearly-spread-appalachia" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;1.5 million acres&lt;/a&gt; of land have been affected by mountaintop removal mining operations, which have buried more than 3,200 kilometers of streams in rubble, a tactic called valley fill.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As groundwater trickles through the jumbled subsurface of a valley fill, it picks up minerals, metals, and salts from the debris, increasing the salt and mineral content of the water downstream. A &lt;a href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/10.1086/676997?seq=1#metadata_info_tab_contents" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;2014 study&lt;/a&gt; by Hitt and colleagues in &lt;em&gt;Freshwater Science&lt;/em&gt; found "fewer species, lower abundances, and less biomass" downstream from mining operations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Trees seem to be the key in kick-starting the healing process. "We've found that restoring trees to the postmining landscape helps restore the hydrologic function" within a matter of years to decades, said &lt;a href="https://nres.ca.uky.edu/person/chris-barton" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Chris Barton&lt;/a&gt;, a forest hydrologist at the University of Kentucky in Lexington. Trees pull a lot of water out of the groundwater system, keeping it from mobilizing minerals, salts, and pollutants.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"As the forest regrows, we're seeing a return to the natural hydrology of these sites, along with improvements in the water quality that [are] needed to support aquatic life," Barton said.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One reforestation project, at a former mountaintop removal mine that was replanted in 2009, went from looking like "the surface of the Moon" to a lush green forest in less than a decade, Barton said. Last year, Barton observed minnows in the stream he and his team created out of the moonscape of rubble.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="new-forever" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="9" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem9"><h3 data-role="headline">New, Forever</h3>&lt;p&gt;Even with restoration and reforestation, the scars left on the landscape by mountaintop removal mining are permanent, Barton said. "Reforesting helps hide the scars, but they're still there. I do wonder about how erosion over geologic timescales will work on those valleys filled with loose, unconsolidated rock," he said. "It seems likely they will erode much faster than the mountains themselves" and may result in deep gorges and holes in the landscape.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In terms of pollution, the legacy of mining in Appalachia will linger essentially forever, said &lt;a href="https://palmerlab.umd.edu/" target="_blank"&gt;Margaret Palmer,&lt;/a&gt; a restoration ecologist at the University of Maryland. "Replanting offers the best hope for restoring stream biodiversity, but no matter what you do, it's crystal clear that mining poisons streams and that mined land will continue to release toxins into the ecosystem in perpetuity."&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-10" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/new-river-gorge" data-id="10" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651100360" data-published-at="1615907674" id="rebelltitem10">&lt;p&gt;With a 300-million-year history, the New is no stranger to perpetuity. The river's deeply entrenched course is likely to stay that way for eons longer, perhaps until the North American continent is reconfigured into a new landmass in a few hundred million years. The New's steady presence may prove crucial to plant and animal species as climate change progresses, Manning said, because species are projected to &lt;a href="https://maps.tnc.org/migrations-in-motion/#4/28.19/-78.93" target="_blank"&gt;migrate north&lt;/a&gt; in response to warming temperatures.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"The New represents one of the most direct avenues for species migration in the Appalachian Mountains," he said. "In the future, I can see the New River Gorge harboring species on their northward migration. I think we'll see how important and resilient the forests of the Appalachians really are, as landscapes and climate continue to change."&lt;/p&gt;</div>

<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="Philadelphia to Dim City Lights to Help Migratory Birds" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/migratory-birds-philadelphia-2651099543.html">
        Philadelphia to Dim City Lights to Help Migratory Birds
    </a>
</h2>
<div class="social-author clearfix">
<div class="social-author__avatar image">
<div class="cover-all image" style='background-image: url("https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNDk3NTM2MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxNjMzMjkzMn0.phOA-i0e2HXFl1wncvNe86b-AMUJyeUOgL7j6VB2l8Q/img.jpg?width=210")'></div>
</div>
<a class="social-author__name" data-type="text" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/u/emilydenny">Emily  Denny</a>
</div>
<div class="social-date">
<span class="social-date__text">15h</span>
</div>
<a class="widget__section js-load-brick-on-click" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/energy-news/">Energy</a>
</div><div class="widget__head"><a aria-label="Philadelphia to Dim City Lights to Help Migratory Birds" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/migratory-birds-philadelphia-2651099543.html">
<div aria-label="Philadelphia to Dim City Lights to Help Migratory Birds" class="widget__image crop-16x9" data-runner-img-hd="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTUyNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxODQyMzE0OX0.xlW7bdebAs4gPqHNzNhAJFEDpMiVQrAYYP5utkTA-TE/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-md="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTUyNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxODQyMzE0OX0.xlW7bdebAs4gPqHNzNhAJFEDpMiVQrAYYP5utkTA-TE/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-sd="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNTgzMTUyNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxODQyMzE0OX0.xlW7bdebAs4gPqHNzNhAJFEDpMiVQrAYYP5utkTA-TE/img.jpg?width=980&amp;quality=85" data-runner-style="padding-bottom: 56.25%;" data-type="image" role="img" style="padding-bottom: 56.25%;"><div class="lazyload-placeholder">

width=336 height=280\n    type=\"doubleclick\"\n    data-slot=\"/5376056/ecowatch_content_8_amp\"\n    data-multi-size=\"320x50,300x250\"\u003e\n\u003c/amp-ad\u003e", "display": true, "new_amp": "\u003camp-ad width=336 height=280\n    type=\"doubleclick\"\n    data-slot=\"/5376056/ecowatch_content_8_amp\"\n    data-multi-size=\"320x50,300x250\"\u003e\n\u003c/amp-ad\u003e", "order": 8}, {"amp": "\u003camp-ad width=336 height=280\n    type=\"doubleclick\"\n    data-slot=\"/5376056/ecowatch_content_9_amp\"\n    data-multi-size=\"320x50,300x250\"\u003e\n\u003c/amp-ad\u003e", "display": true, "new_amp": "\u003camp-ad width=336 height=280\n    type=\"doubleclick\"\n    data-slot=\"/5376056/ecowatch_content_9_amp\"\n    data-multi-size=\"320x50,300x250\"\u003e\n\u003c/amp-ad\u003e", "order": 9}, {"amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "display": true, "new_amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "order": 10}], "adsOrder": [2]}
        </script><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>On the morning of October 2, Stephen Maciejewski, an Audubon Pennsylvania volunteer, expected to tally a few dead<a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/birds" target="_blank"> birds</a> in Philadelphia during their peak migration season. But instead, he found hundreds of dead birds, <a href="https://www.cnn.com/2020/10/09/us/1500-birds-collide-skyscraper-scn-trnd/index.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">CNN reported</a>. </p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-1" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/migratory-birds-philadelphia" data-id="1" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099543" data-published-at="1615904412" id="rebelltitem1">&lt;p&gt;"I've never seen anything like this," Maciejewski told CNN in October. "There were birds everywhere, and they were all dead."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Overnight, an estimated 1,000 to 1,500 birds collided with &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/cities" target="_blank"&gt;city&lt;/a&gt; buildings in a three-and-a-half block radius, &lt;a href="https://www.inquirer.com/science/climate/philadelphia-birds-lights-out-audubon-20210311.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;according to The Philadelphia Inquirer&lt;/a&gt;. Poor weather conditions and bright building lights were to blame for the city's largest mass-bird-collision event in 70 years, &lt;a href="https://apnews.com/article/science-philadelphia-climate-change-birds-ae7b8dc52663edd720596445f76aa105" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;AP reported&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In response to growing concern about migratory birds and illuminated city skylines, a coalition of organizations are teaming up to form Lights Out Philly  a voluntary program aimed at preventing building collisions during spring and fall migrations. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The coalition, made up of the Audubon Mid-Atlantic, the Academy of Natural Sciences of Drexel University, the Delaware Valley Ornithological Club and two local Audubon chapters, is calling on building managers and tenants to turn off unnecessary lights from April 1 through May 31 and from Aug. 15 to Nov. 15, between midnight and 6 a.m. &lt;a href="https://apnews.com/article/science-philadelphia-climate-change-birds-ae7b8dc52663edd720596445f76aa105" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;according to the AP&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-2" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/migratory-birds-philadelphia" data-id="2" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099543" data-published-at="1615904412" id="rebelltitem2">&lt;p&gt;"We have some early adopters and the list is approaching 20 buildings, many of which are iconic and very recognizable members of the Philadelphia skyline, such as One and Two Liberty Place, Comcast Technology Center and Comcast Center, Mellon Bank Building and all of Brandywine Realty Trust's Center City and University City buildings," said Kristine Kiphorn, executive director of the Building Owners and Managers Association of Philadelphia, according to the AP.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;"We get to do our part in the community to help preserve the bird population, and we get to conserve &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/energy-news/" target="_blank"&gt;energy&lt;/a&gt; at the same time, saving money for our tenants and our assets."&lt;/p&gt;About 70 percent of North American birds are migratory  of them, more than 80 percent migrate at night, &lt;a href="https://www.inquirer.com/opinion/commentary/birds-center-city-philadelphia-lights-out-buildings-20201021.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;according to an op-ed in the Philadelphia Inquirer&lt;/a&gt; by Robert M. Peck, a senior fellow at the Academy of Natural Sciences of Drexel University, and Keith Russell, program manager of Urban Conservation at Audubon Pennsylvania. Bright lights can confuse and disorient migrating birds, causing them to fly into buildings.&lt;br&gt;&lt;br&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Many of the deaths that occur at night go unnoticed, cleaned up by vigilant maintenance crews before the morning commute begins. Fortunately, this is a problem that we can do something about," Peck and Russell wrote in The Philadelphia Inquirer.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-3" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/migratory-birds-philadelphia" data-id="3" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099543" data-published-at="1615904412" id="rebelltitem3">&lt;p&gt;Simply turning lights off at night could reduce bird deaths by up to 80 percent, Lights Out Philly wrote in a &lt;a href="https://6fab4b1f-ba30-4def-92e3-5b4a4fcdcb00.filesusr.com/ugd/7b1c1d_6196c16a386749febea78f6c500ea598.pdf" target="_blank"&gt;release.&lt;/a&gt; The program could also reduce annual energy costs for building managers and tenants. In Toronto, for example, one municipal building under the Lights Out program saved more than $200,000 in a single year.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Up to one billion birds collide with U.S. buildings every year, &lt;a href="https://www.inquirer.com/opinion/commentary/birds-center-city-philadelphia-lights-out-buildings-20201021.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;according to&lt;/a&gt; Peck and Russell. Building collisions are a critical problem for some species that are also increasingly threatened by the changing climate. &lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;"According to the most recent climate research conducted by Audubon, many of the 300-plus bird species that occur in Philadelphia, such as the Ovenbird and the Black-throated Blue Warbler, are among the hundreds of bird species that are now at an increased risk of &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/extinction" target="_blank"&gt;extinction&lt;/a&gt; in North America because of &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/climate-change" target="_blank"&gt;climate change&lt;/a&gt;," Russell told &lt;a href="https://www.audubon.org/news/its-time-lights-out-philadelphia" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Audubon&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;"By participating in Bird Safe Philly's Lights Out program, Philadelphia will not only help to protect these birds by further reducing energy consumption, which can slow climate change, but by also reducing the nocturnal lighting that threatens so many of these birds."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Philadelphia is joining 33 other cities in the Lights Out program, including Boston, New York City, Atlanta and Washington D.C., the AP reported.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;"We are heartened by all the efforts in our community to join together in this critical initiative to save so many birds from unnecessary harm and even death," Scott Cooper, president and CEO of the Academy of Natural Sciences, told The Philadelphia Inquirer. "A simple thing like turning out lights can help thousands of birds safely navigate our challenging urban environment."&lt;/p&gt;</div>

"adsOrder": [2]}
        </script><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>By Jessica Corbett</p><p>A diverse coalition of progressive and <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/indigenous-peoples">Indigenous</a> figures and organizations on Monday celebrated the Senate's confirmation of Deb Haaland to head the Department of the Interior  making the New Mexico Democrat and Green New Deal supporter the first Native American Cabinet secretary in U.S. history.</p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-1" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="1" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem1">&lt;p&gt;She was confirmed by a &lt;a href="https://www.senate.gov/legislative/LIS/roll_call_lists/roll_call_vote_cfm.cfm?congress=117&amp;amp;session=1&amp;amp;vote=00119" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;51-40 vote&lt;/a&gt; in which only a handful of Republicans joined with all members of the Democratic caucus who were present; nine lawmakers, including three Democrats, did not vote. "I look forward to collaborating with all of you," Haaland &lt;a href="https://twitter.com/DebHaalandNM/status/1371589252794830848" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;said&lt;/a&gt; in a tweet directed at senators. "I am ready to serve."&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-2" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="2" data-is-image="True" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem2">&lt;img lazy-loadable="true" src="https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vbWVkaWEucmJsLm1zL2ltYWdlP3U9JTJGbWVkaWElMkZFd2piZG9fVzhBOFo1VkEuanBnJTNBbWVkaXVtJmhvPWh0dHBzJTNBJTJGJTJGcGJzLnR3aW1nLmNvbSZzPTEzOCZoPWMwZTI3NDIxNTgwZTlhYTliNjljZmM5Y2EwZjA0M2Q0NGY2ODQ0M2IwYTM0NDZjY2FhNjdkMDU5YWIxYWI1ZGQmc2l6ZT05ODB4JmM9MjcwMTgwMjY5IiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MTYyMzM3OH0.Y5cV2tGO3jSjahzSQTXueAe23pZ7TGog59jaF6Oc2mc/img.jpg" id="4609c" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="0fdcd9d8f8c81d2b39db05e6c85c6029"  /&gt;<small class="image-media media-caption">#DebForInterior on Twitter</small><small class="image-media media-photo-credit"><p><a href="https://twitter.com/jnoisecat/status/1371587945707286531" target="_blank">twitter.com</a></p>
</small></div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-3" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="3" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem3">&lt;p&gt;While climate justice and Indigenous rights advocates have &lt;a href="https://www.commondreams.org/news/2020/12/17/perfect-choice-progressives-applaud-biden-pick-deb-haaland-interior-secretary" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;praised &lt;/a&gt;President Joe Biden's selection of the congresswoman to lead a department that oversees hundreds of million of as "a perfect choice," both &lt;a href="https://www.commondreams.org/news/2021/02/24/glad-thats-settled-manchin-backs-haaland-interior" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;conservative Democrats&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://www.commondreams.org/news/2021/03/09/schumer-vows-push-through-republican-obstruction-and-confirm-deb-haaland-interior" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Republicans&lt;/a&gt; have raised concerns about her positions on &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/fossil-fuels"&gt;fossil fuels&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"It's clear that Big Oil was afraid of her confirmation  and for good reason," said David Turnbull, strategic communications director at Oil Change International. "The nine senators that voted against her confirmation on the Energy and Natural Resources Committee have on average accepted nearly $900,000 from Big Oil, Coal, and Gas over the course of their careers."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"The confirmation of Deb Haaland as secretary of the interior is a tremendous win for Indigenous communities, the waters, parks, and lands across our country, and the climate," he added. "Haaland is a proven climate champion, and will usher in a new era of climate leadership in the Department of the Interior, reversing the tide of the last four years of dirty energy policies enacted by the Trump administration."&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-4" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="4" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem4">&lt;p&gt;Food &amp;amp; Water Watch executive director Wenonah Hauter similarly &lt;a href="https://www.commondreams.org/newswire/2021/03/15/haalands-historic-confirmation-points-way-fracking-ban" target="_blank"&gt;welcomed&lt;/a&gt; Haaland's appointment as an opportunity to depart from the planet-wrecking agenda of former President Donald Trump  whose attacks on climate and environmental policies led some campaigners and experts to back Biden's presidential bid.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Deb Haaland's historic, pathbreaking confirmation is a victory for Indigenous communities, for the climate movement, and for everyone who wants to undo the Trump-era assault on our air and water," said Hauter. "The massive outpouring of public support for Haaland is a testament to her uncompromising record and her clear commitment to ending the exploitation of public lands by fossil fuel corporations."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Biden's unambiguous call to end fracking on public lands must now become a priority for the White House," she said. "There is no one better to lead on this issue than Deb Haaland, who understands that our transition away from fossil fuels is an environmental justice priority and a climate necessity."&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-5" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="5" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem5">&lt;div id="4662e" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="19d1c5349ab2903fa67d1b4957b65d4e"&gt;&lt;blockquote class="twitter-tweet twitter-custom-tweet" data-twitter-tweet-id="1371592194126094338" data-partner="rebelmouse"&gt;&lt;div style="margin:1em 0"&gt;Deb Haaland is now the first Indigenous person to lead the Department of the Interior- ushering in a new era of Ind https://t.co/8SxYI6TG97&lt;/div&gt; &amp;mdash; Sunrise Movement  (@Sunrise Movement )&lt;a href="https://twitter.com/sunrisemvmt/statuses/1371592194126094338"&gt;1615848036.0&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-6" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="6" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem6">&lt;p&gt;"In Congress, Rep. Haaland has demonstrated a deep concern for environmental justice, conservation, and climate change," &lt;a href="https://www.commondreams.org/newswire/2021/03/15/science-group-eager-see-haaland-lead-department-interior" target="_blank"&gt;said&lt;/a&gt; Kathleen Rest, executive director of the Union of Concerned Scientists. "We look forward to working with the new secretary of the interior to protect our nation's ecosystems, cultural heritage, and the climate  for the benefit of communities across the country, including tribes."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Varshini Prakash, executive director of the youth-led Sunrise Movement, lauded Biden for listening to "the Indigenous movement groups and allies that pushed for her confirmation, despite the Senate Republicans' unfair, aggressive campaign against her."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Haaland is not only one of the first co-sponsors of the Green New Deal," Prakash pointed out, "but she believes that achieving Indigenous sovereignty over stolen land is instrumental to transforming our economy and stopping the climate crisis."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Now, she must wield her power to crack down on fossil fuel corporations and evict companies that drill for oil on public lands and in public waters," the activist added. "We are excited to see the progress she makes and will be standing with her as she pushes us closer to the vision of a Green New Deal."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sarah McMillan, WildEarth Guardians' conservation director, and Adam Kolton, executive director of Alaska Wilderness League, highlighted the significance of not only Haaland's well established positions on dirty energy and land management, but also the perspective she brings as an enrolled member of the Laguna Pueblo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"We congratulate Secretary Deb Haaland and celebrate this historic and momentous day when the Department of Interior, which manages more than 450 million acres and houses the Bureau of Indian Affairs, finally has an Indigenous woman to lead the way," said McMillan. "As our country is finally awakening to the injustices that Indigenous people have endured, and in the throes of a climate and nature crisis, we must chart a new path after four years of out-of-control exploitation and devastation."&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-8" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="8" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem8">&lt;div id="79da9" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="1881a759929b652c4feb5021b4e311a1"&gt;&lt;blockquote class="twitter-tweet twitter-custom-tweet" data-twitter-tweet-id="1371594457208872963" data-partner="rebelmouse"&gt;&lt;div style="margin:1em 0"&gt;Deb Haaland defended Native Americans and her views on renewable energy during her Cabinet confirmation hearing.

S https://t.co/Upt1tIVmGg&lt;/div&gt; &amp;mdash; AJ+ (@AJ+)&lt;a href="https://twitter.com/ajplus/statuses/1371594457208872963"&gt;1615848576.0&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-9" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/haaland-interior-indigenous-conservation-climate" data-id="9" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651099676" data-published-at="1615904238" id="rebelltitem9">&lt;p&gt;As Kolton put it: "We cannot right the wrongs of colonization or undo the history of violence toward and the displacement of Indigenous peoples in our country. But the confirmation of the first Native American to lead any U.S. Cabinet agency, particularly one that is responsible for the stewardship of millions of acres of once stolen and now public land, is a historic and hopeful moment."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Haaland brings to the job an understanding of the Native American experience and a deep personal connection and commitment to preserving the lands and waters that sustain people and communities across the country," he added. "She was the first U.S. representative to champion the need to pursue an ambitious national goal of conserving 30% of U.S. lands and waters by 2030, and as interior secretary she will play a critical role in implementing President Biden's ambitious conservation agenda, confronting the climate crisis, and advancing the cause of environmental justice."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A host of conservation groups in the state she represented in Congress  including New Mexico Wildlife Federation, New Mexico Wild, and Conservation Voters New Mexico  also applauded Haaland's confirmation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"The global climate crisis is attributed to settler colonialism and the exploitation of natural resources cultivated from stolen Indigenous landscapes and the mismanagement of those resources," said Pueblo Action Alliance director Julia Bernal. "There needs to be a paradigm shift and having a Pueblo Indigenous feminist perspective in this Cabinet position could instill a lot of hope for meaningful tribal consultation and more importantly tribal consent."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Bernal explained that "Haaland will bring that worldview into land and water management practices that will work towards a just transition to a cleaner energy economy and more equitable approaches to better frontline and Indigenous communities who have suffered from the presence of the oil and gas industry."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Noting the department's widely criticized history on interacting with tribes, she added that "places like Chaco Canyon and Bears Ears and the waterways that supply Indigenous people in the Southwest could have longer standing chances if they are managed through a Pueblo Indigenous feminist perspective that implement[s] core values like respect and reciprocity and give[s] personhood to our waterways."&lt;/p&gt;</div>

<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="New Polar Bear Hotel Criticized for Animal Cruelty" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/polar-bear-hotel-china-animal-cruelty-2651088401.html">
        New Polar Bear Hotel Criticized for Animal Cruelty
    </a>
</h2>

<div class="social-author__avatar image">

"adsOrder": [2]}
        </script><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>A new hotel is sparking controversy with its central conceit: a <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/polar-bears" target="_self">polar bear</a> enclosure visible from all of its 21 rooms. </p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-1" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/polar-bear-hotel-china-animal-cruelty" data-id="1" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088401" data-published-at="1615829828" id="rebelltitem1">&lt;p&gt;The Polar Bear Hotel opened Friday in the city of Harbin, &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/china" target="_self"&gt;China&lt;/a&gt;, as &lt;a href="https://www.reuters.com/article/china-polar-hotel/chinese-polar-bear-hotel-opens-to-full-bookings-criticism-idUSL1N2LB04Q" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank"&gt;Reuters reported&lt;/a&gt;. It is fully booked through a trial period, but has been widely criticized by &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/animal-welfare" target="_self"&gt;animal welfare&lt;/a&gt; advocates.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Polar bears belong in the &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/arctic" target="_self"&gt;Arctic&lt;/a&gt;, not in zoos or glass boxes in aquariums  and certainly not in hotels," People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals (PETA) Asia senior vice president Jason Baker told Reuters. "Polar bears are active for up to 18 hours a day in nature, roaming home ranges that can span thousands of miles, where they enjoy a real life."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A video shared by the South China Morning Post shows two bears in a small enclosure with harsh lighting, artificial ice and small pools of water.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-2" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/polar-bear-hotel-china-animal-cruelty" data-id="2" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088401" data-published-at="1615829828" id="rebelltitem2">&lt;span style="display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;" class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="0d5d64cf5f1fbf2f9b9f5ab306d3d29e"&gt;&lt;iframe lazy-loadable="true" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/bMoWdFL34Vs?rel=0" width="100%" height="auto"  frameborder="0" scrolling="no" style="position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;"&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;&lt;/span&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-3" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/polar-bear-hotel-china-animal-cruelty" data-id="3" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088401" data-published-at="1615829828" id="rebelltitem3">&lt;p&gt;The hotel is part of the Harbin Polarland theme park, according to Reuters. Harbin is the capital city of the northeasternmost Chinese province of Heilongjiang. It hosts a famous ice-carving festival, and the new hotel is shaped like an igloo, according to an &lt;a href="https://www.theguardian.com/world/2021/mar/12/chinese-hotel-with-polar-bear-enclosure-opens-to-outrage" target="_blank"&gt;AFP story published by The Guardian&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Harbin Polarland spokeswoman Yang Liu told Reuters that the indoor enclosure is only part of the bears' habitat, and that they also have an outdoor area they are allowed to access when the temperature and air quality are appropriate. However, the hotel advertises 24-hour viewing access to the bears.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Whether you're eating, playing or sleeping, polar bears will keep you company," Harbin Polarland wrote on WeChat Thursday, as Reuters reported.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For this opportunity, guests pay $290.10 to $351.56 a night, and Liu said interest in the hotel was "very high."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, activists are calling on guests to avoid a hotel that makes money "from &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/animals/" target="_self"&gt;animals&lt;/a&gt;' misery," AFP reported.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-4" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/polar-bear-hotel-china-animal-cruelty" data-id="4" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088401" data-published-at="1615829828" id="rebelltitem4">&lt;p&gt;China has faced global criticism for animal rights' abuses, including the use of &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/endangered-species" target="_self"&gt;endangered species&lt;/a&gt; in traditional medicines and the treatment of animals in zoos and circuses.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Gaps in China's &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/wildlife" target="_self"&gt;wildlife&lt;/a&gt; protection law allows businesses to exploit animals without any concern for their welfare," an anonymous spokesman for China Animal Protection Network told AFP.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, there are signs that things are shifting. Authorities &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/china-wildlife-trade-ban-2645320590.html" target="_self"&gt;banned&lt;/a&gt; the use of wildlife for food following the outbreak of the coronavirus pandemic. Further, in January of 2020, a video showing a pig being forced to bungee jump at a Chinese amusement park earned widespread social media backlash within the country, as &lt;a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/bungee-jumping-pig-china-2644879655.html" target="_self"&gt;EcoWatch reported at the time&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"The Chinese public's angry response should be a wake-up call to China's policy-makers to implement animal protection laws immediately," Baker told BBC News in response.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The new hotel has also earned criticism from Chinese social media users.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Looking at a polar bear slowly having a mental breakdown up close? Such a small place, with so much lighting and so many windows. You should first try putting humans in," one commenter wrote, as South China Morning Post reported.&lt;/p&gt;</div>

"order": 9}, {"amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "display": true, "new_amp": "\u003cdiv class=\"placeholder-last-ad\"\u003e\u003c/div\u003e", "order": 10}], "adsOrder": [2]}
        </script><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>Art Zilleruelo's poetry is often inspired by nature. Growing up, he wandered the hills of rural Pennsylvania.</p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-1" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/pennsylvania-poet-coal-mining" data-id="1" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088729" data-published-at="1615827919" id="rebelltitem1">&lt;iframe style="border: none" src="//html5-player.libsyn.com/embed/episode/id/17955269/height/45/theme/standard/thumbnail/yes/direction/backward/" height="45" width="100%" scrolling="no"  allowfullscreen webkitallowfullscreen mozallowfullscreen oallowfullscreen msallowfullscreen&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;
</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-2" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/pennsylvania-poet-coal-mining" data-id="2" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088729" data-published-at="1615827919" id="rebelltitem2">&lt;p&gt;"It's still possible to do things like get lost in the woods here if you want to," he says. "It's still possible to find places where the night sky is completely unpolluted by artificial light."&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But the region Zilleruelo is from is known more for coal than trees and stars. And in his recent book of poems, called "&lt;a target="_blank" href="https://bookshop.org/books/toothsome/9781950380657"&gt;Toothsome&lt;/a&gt;," he grapples with this legacy.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-4" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/pennsylvania-poet-coal-mining" data-id="4" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088729" data-published-at="1615827919" id="rebelltitem4">&lt;p&gt;He says the economic benefits of coal mining came with a big cost for many miners like his grandfather and great-grandfather.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;"Both ended up succumbing to complications from what gets called black lung disease which is the accumulation of coal sediment in the lungs," Zilleruelo says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Strip mining has also scarred the earth. And in the town of Centralia, a coal mine fire has burned underground for more than 50 years.&lt;/p&gt;</div>
<div class="rebellt-item is--desktop col1" data-basename="particle-5" data-href="https://www.ecowatch.com/pennsylvania-poet-coal-mining" data-id="5" data-is-image="False" data-post-id="2651088729" data-published-at="1615827919" id="rebelltitem5">&lt;p&gt;In his poetry, such as this example from his new book, Zilleruelo wrestles with these losses and people's role in harming the climate and land.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;"Who now can doubt that the earth hides fires&lt;br&gt;That the land can speak through many mouths&lt;br&gt;That some dark mountain husbandry&lt;br&gt;Has converted this forest to a larder&lt;br&gt;And these fields to an oven."&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;</div>

window.REBELMOUSE_TASKS_QUEUE.push(function(){

    
        function replaceHoursText() {
    var date = document.querySelectorAll('.post-date, .social-date__text');
    date = Object.values(date);
    for(var i=0; i<date.length; i++){
      var str = date[i].innerText;
      var dateElement = date[i];
      var oldFormat = str.split(" ");
      
      if (str.includes("ago")){
      	continue;
      }
      
      if(str.length > 3 && isNaN(oldFormat[0])){
      	continue;
      }
      
      if (str.length > 3){
        //dealing with months
        var newFormat;
        
        if(oldFormat.length==2){
        	newFormat = oldFormat[1].substring(0, 3) + " " + oldFormat[0];
        } else {
            newFormat = oldFormat[1] + " " + oldFormat[0] + ", "+ oldFormat[2]
        } 
        dateElement.innerText = newFormat;
      } else if (str == "1h") {
         dateElement.innerText = str.replace('h', ' hour ago');
      } else if (str.includes("h")) {
         dateElement.innerText = str.replace('h', ' hours ago');
      } else {
         dateElement.innerText = str.replace('m', ' minutes ago');
      }
    }
}

 replaceHoursText();
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function() {
    replaceHoursText();
});

setInterval(replaceHoursText, 3000);
    

});

});</script>
